    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer13948993907317424906">Accused of mocking the inviolate codes of Islam, the Persian poet and sage Omar Khayyam fortuitously finds sympathy with the very man who is to judge his alleged crimes. Recognising genuis, the judge decides to spare him and gives him instead a small, blank book, encouraging him to confine his thoughts to it alone. Thus begins the seamless blend of fact and fiction that is</span>
  <span id="freeText13948993907317424906" style="display:none">Accused of mocking the inviolate codes of Islam, the Persian poet and sage Omar Khayyam fortuitously finds sympathy with the very man who is to judge his alleged crimes. Recognising genuis, the judge decides to spare him and gives him instead a small, blank book, encouraging him to confine his thoughts to it alone. Thus begins the seamless blend of fact and fiction that is <i>Samarkand</i>. Vividly re-creating the history of the manuscript of the Rubaiyaat of Omar Khayyam, Amin Maalouf spans continents and centuries with breathtaking vision: the dusky exoticism of 11th-century Persia, with its poetesses and assassins; the same country's struggles nine hundred years later, seen through the eyes of an American academic obsessed with finding the original manuscript; and the fated maiden voyage of the Titanic, whose tragedy led to the Rubaiyaat's final resting place - all are brought to life with keen assurance by this gifted and award-winning writer.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13948993907317424906" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer9635191546567489762">Samarcande = ‎Samarchande = SamarKand‬, Amin Maalouf<br/>Samarkand is a 1988 historical fiction novel by the French-Lebanese writer Amin Maalouf. The first half of the story is set in Persia (present day Iran) and Central Asia in the 11th century, and revolves around the scientist, philosopher, and poet Omar Khayyám. It recounts the creation of his Rubaiyat throughout the history of the Seljuk Empire, his interactions with historical figures such as Vizir Nizam al-Mulk and Hassan al-Sabbah of the ord</span>
  <span id="freeText9635191546567489762" style="display:none">Samarcande = ‎Samarchande = SamarKand‬, Amin Maalouf<br />Samarkand is a 1988 historical fiction novel by the French-Lebanese writer Amin Maalouf. The first half of the story is set in Persia (present day Iran) and Central Asia in the 11th century, and revolves around the scientist, philosopher, and poet Omar Khayyám. It recounts the creation of his Rubaiyat throughout the history of the Seljuk Empire, his interactions with historical figures such as Vizir Nizam al-Mulk and Hassan al-Sabbah of the order of the Assassins, and his love affair with a female poet of the Samarkand court. The second half of the story documents the efforts of a fictional American named Benjamin O. Lesage to obtain the (fictional) original copy of the Rubaiyat, witnessing Persian history throughout the Persian Constitutional Revolution of 1905-1907, only to lose this manuscript in the sinking of the RMS Titanic.<br /><br />تاریخ نخستین خوانش: روز شانزدهم ماه آگوست سال 2001 میلادی<br />عنوان: سمرقند؛ نویسنده: امین مالوف (معلوف)؛ مترجم: محمد قاضی؛ مشخصات نشر: تهران، زرین، 1379، در 563 ص، شابک: 9644072073؛ موضوع: داستان دستنویس سرّی خیام، خواجه نظام الملک، و حسن صباح، داستانهای نویسندگان فرانسوی - سده 20 م<br />عنوان: سمرقند؛ نویسنده: امین مالوف (معلوف)؛ مترجم: کاظم شیوانظری؛ مشخصات نشر: تهران، دستان، چاپ نخست 1374، در 422 ص، شابک: 9646555029؛ <br />عنوان: سمرقند؛ نویسنده: امین مالوف (معلوف)؛ مترجم: احمد سادات عقیلی؛ مشخصات نشر: تهران، مدبر، 1381، در 356 ص، شابک: 9646631282؛ <br />عنوان: سمرقند؛ نویسنده: امین مالوف (معلوف)؛ مترجم: عبدالرضا هوشنگ مهدوی؛ مشخصات نشر: تهران، نشرگفتار، 1381، در 411 ص، شابک: 9646631282؛ <br /><br />سمرقند، کتابی داستانی ـ تاریخی، درباره‌ ی برخی از شخصیت‌ها، و جریانات اجتماعیِ ایران، در دو دوره‌ ی تاریخی است (سده پنجم هجری، و سده چهاردهم هجری، برابر با سده یازدهم و بیستم میلادی)؛ و در این میان شاید حضور «سمرقند»، برای دو تن از شخصیت‌های داستان، که در دو دوره‌ ی جداگانه می‌زیستند (عمر خیام نیشابوری و بنجامین عمر لوساژ) به منزله‌ ی نمادِ رویایی مشترک از شهر ـ جهانی باشد، که هستیِ افسانه‌ ای آن، بر واقعیتش برتری داشته است. عشق به «خیام» و رباعیات او «بنجامین عمر لوساژ»، روزنامه‌ نگار و پژوهنده ی آمریکایی را به ایران می‌کشاند. او پس از پشت سر گذاشتن رویدادهای بسیار، سرانجام این دست‌نوشته گرانقدر را به دست آورده، و تصمیم دارد تا آن را با خود به آمریکا ببرد. اما با غرق شدن کشتی تایتانیک، او به همراه همسرش و نیز صندوقچه ی طلایی دست‌نوشته به ژرفای اقیانوس می‌روند. ا. شربیانی</span>
  <a data-text-id="9635191546567489762" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<span id="freeTextContainer4355972080298519519">I wrote down 12 quotes from this book when I read it back in 1999 -- clearly I loved it. Of course, I am sure that a great deal of that love came from being in Central Asia at the time and having been to Samarkand. It's a fictional story about the Rubiyaat and Omar Khayyam.<br/><br/>p. 26<br/>"Time ... has two dimensions, its length is measured by the rhythm of the sun but its depth by the rhythm of passion."<br/><br/>p. 81<br/>"...the qualities needed to govern are not those which are needed in order to accede to power. In </span>
  <span id="freeText4355972080298519519" style="display:none">I wrote down 12 quotes from this book when I read it back in 1999 -- clearly I loved it. Of course, I am sure that a great deal of that love came from being in Central Asia at the time and having been to Samarkand. It's a fictional story about the Rubiyaat and Omar Khayyam.<br /><br />p. 26<br />"Time ... has two dimensions, its length is measured by the rhythm of the sun but its depth by the rhythm of passion."<br /><br />p. 81<br />"...the qualities needed to govern are not those which are needed in order to accede to power. In order to run things smoothly, one must forget oneself and only be interested in others -- particularly the most unfortunate; to get into power, one must be the greediest of men, think only of oneself and be ready to crush one's closest friends. I, however, will not crush anyone."<br /><br />p. 108<br />"In my opinion, any cause which involves killing no longer attracts me. It becomes unattractive to me, it becomes sordid and debased, no matter how beautiful it may have been. No cause can be just when it allies itself to death."<br /><br />p. 299<br />"It was said that a half-mad king had condemned Nasruddin (Hodja)to death for having stolen an ass. Just as he was about to be led off for execution he exclaimed: 'That beast is in reality my brother. A magician made him look like that, but if he were entrusted to me for a year I would teach him to speak like us again!' Intrigued, the monarch made the accused repeat his promise before decreeing: 'Very well! But if within one year from today the ass does not speak, you will be executed!' As he went out, Nasruddin was accosted by his wife: 'How can you make a promise like that? You know very well that this ass will not speak.' 'Of course I know,' replied Nasruddin, 'but during the year the king might die, the ass might or even I might.'"</span>

    <a title="Sue" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3642045-sue"><img alt="Sue" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1274829293p2/3642045.jpg" /></a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

      MENA

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3642045-sue?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer230769537" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4205356370959830137">I learned of this book through the MiddleEast/NorthAfrica group here at Goodreads. Otherwise I would have missed out on a very interesting and fascinating trip through medieval and late 19th/early 20th century Persia or Iran. Though this is historical fiction, I feel as if I have been given an introduction into the Iranian past, an introduction that helps to explain some of the present. <br/><br/>The primary focus is Omar Khayyam and his book, the Rubaiyaat. Khayyam was a philosopher, poet, consultant to </span>
  <span id="freeText4205356370959830137" style="display:none">I learned of this book through the MiddleEast/NorthAfrica group here at Goodreads. Otherwise I would have missed out on a very interesting and fascinating trip through medieval and late 19th/early 20th century Persia or Iran. Though this is historical fiction, I feel as if I have been given an introduction into the Iranian past, an introduction that helps to explain some of the present. <br /><br />The primary focus is Omar Khayyam and his book, the Rubaiyaat. Khayyam was a philosopher, poet, consultant to leaders, astrologer, scientist of his day. The first half of the book outlines his life, the politics of his time and some of his writings. In the second half, we are transported to more modern times, to a Persia in a state of unrest, and to a search for the Rubaiyaat which has not been seen for centuries. <br /><br />This book was originally written in French but I must agree with others who have praised the English translation. It flows and draws the reader (at least this reader) in. </span>
  <a data-text-id="4205356370959830137" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8110617-ilhem?shelf=read-in-french">read-in-french</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer601321925" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14221286265346724099"><br/><i>“At the bottom of the Atlantic, there is a book. I am going to tell you its history. (…) Six years after the event, I am still obsessed by this object of flesh and ink, whose unworthy guardian I was“</i> - Benjamin Omar Lesage.<br/><br/>Amin Maalouf said about his work as a novelist that he tried to build positive myths. That describes <i>Samarkand</i> so well!<br/>In this book, he gave Omar Khayyam’s <i>Rubaiyat</i> a long lost manuscript with Persia’s history in its marge, found by his fictional narrator Benjamin O. Lesage on</span>
  <span id="freeText14221286265346724099" style="display:none"><br><i>“At the bottom of the Atlantic, there is a book. I am going to tell you its history. (…) Six years after the event, I am still obsessed by this object of flesh and ink, whose unworthy guardian I was“</i> - Benjamin Omar Lesage.<br><br>Amin Maalouf said about his work as a novelist that he tried to build positive myths. That describes <i>Samarkand</i> so well!<br>In this book, he gave Omar Khayyam’s <i>Rubaiyat</i> a long lost manuscript with Persia’s history in its marge, found by his fictional narrator Benjamin O. Lesage only to be lost again and become a priceless sunken treasure.<br><br>Like Vartan the Armenian who annoted Omar’s poems, Amin Maalouf imagined a biography as if the quatrains had revealed a secret from now on known by him only and intertwined it with the fates of Nizam al-Mulk, 'Order of the Kingdom' and Hassan Sabbah , the 'Old Man of the Mountain' who founded the order of the Assassins.<br><br>The (hi)story begins when Omar Khayyam, polymath, mathematician, philosopher, astronomer and poet appears before a judge who not only releases him but gives him a manuscript to record his thoughts, to keep them secure and him safe until the world is ready to receive them. It ends after the Persian Constitutional Revolution. <br><br><i>Samarkand</i> is a novel but it is first and foremost a chronicle where the author reflects upon History, power, violence, identity, the relationship between East and West…<br>I wasn’t so much enthralled by the fiction as deeply interested in the underlying essay and delighted to stop once in a while, just to take the time to savour Omar Khayyam’s quatrains which are beyond rating for me.<br><br><i>Note : I read Samarkan in its original French version but it is also available in an English edition, translated by Russell Harris. It is where the quote comes from.</i><br><br><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/142395.Samarkand" title="Samarkand by Amin Maalouf" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/books/1327995121s/142395.jpg" alt="Samarkand by Amin Maalouf" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="14221286265346724099" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer2413288666368689863">This went over my head. <br/><br/> I did learn about the Sufi poet, sage, astronomer, mathematician, Omar Khayyam (1048-1131) of 11th century Persia and his famed <i> Rubaiyaat</i>, about life during the Seljuk empire before the Mongol invasion, about Nizam ul Mulk, Hassan Sabbah, the founder of the Order of the Assassins, and later about the Persian democratic struggle for a constitutional government. That is why I am giving this book two stars. I did learn something, but I also had to spend quite a bit of time</span>
  <span id="freeText2413288666368689863" style="display:none">This went over my head. <br /><br /> I did learn about the Sufi poet, sage, astronomer, mathematician, Omar Khayyam (1048-1131) of 11th century Persia and his famed <i> Rubaiyaat</i>, about life during the Seljuk empire before the Mongol invasion, about Nizam ul Mulk, Hassan Sabbah, the founder of the Order of the Assassins, and later about the Persian democratic struggle for a constitutional government. That is why I am giving this book two stars. I did learn something, but I also had to spend quite a bit of time searching on the net and in other literature! I was so often confused.<br /><br />I did not enjoy the mix of fact and fiction. The fictional parts felt unbelievable. I didn’t enjoy the language used; it is wordy; it is highbrow. The story is told, rather than shown. The love affairs did not move me. I felt nothing for the characters. No, I did not enjoy this book; it was a chore. I am the oddball out! Perhaps you should go read somebody else’s adulatory review!</span>

<div id="review_1718985916" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1718985916" />
    <a title="Sidharth Vardhan" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/58296635-sidharth-vardhan"><img alt="Sidharth Vardhan" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1595750367p2/58296635.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1718985916?book_show_action=true">May 05, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sidharth Vardhan" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sidharth Vardhan" href="/user/show/58296635-sidharth-vardhan">Sidharth Vardhan</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/58296635-sidharth-vardhan?shelf=2-asia">2-asia</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/58296635-sidharth-vardhan?shelf=list-1001">list-1001</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1718985916" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1756318811747443799"><blockquote>
  <i><br/>"Omar Khayyam mourned his disciple with the same dignity, the same resignation and the same discreet agony as he had mourned other friends. ‘We were drinking the same wine, but they got drunk two or three rounds before me.’" <br/></i>
</blockquote><br/>Among other things, this book has among its motifs - Omar Khayyam, Hassan-i Sabbah, Persian liberation efforts at the beginning of 20th century, Titanic, Mongols etc.<br/><br/>Have you ever detests the 'x' of algebra during your math classes, well Omar Khayyam is the source of tha</span>
  <span id="freeText1756318811747443799" style="display:none"><blockquote>
  <i><br />"Omar Khayyam mourned his disciple with the same dignity, the same resignation and the same discreet agony as he had mourned other friends. ‘We were drinking the same wine, but they got drunk two or three rounds before me.’" <br /></i>
</blockquote><br />Among other things, this book has among its motifs - Omar Khayyam, Hassan-i Sabbah, Persian liberation efforts at the beginning of 20th century, Titanic, Mongols etc.<br /><br />Have you ever detests the 'x' of algebra during your math classes, well Omar Khayyam is the source of that 'x'. <br /><blockquote>
  <i><br />"to represent the unknown in this treatise on algebra, Khayyam used the Arabic term shay, which means thing. This word, spelled xay in Spanish scientific works, was gradually replaced by its first letter, x, which became the universal symbol for the unknown."<br /></i>
</blockquote><br />He was a polymath - a true polymath, not one of the modern-day self-claimed ones who learn basics of many fields without mastering any. Omar wrote thesis in maths and astronomy and wrote incredible poems famous all over the world - and that had a really strong influence on sufi poems - even though he himself was far less submissive to God: <br />blockquote&gt;<i><br />"I am not one of those for whom faith is simply fear of judgement. How do I pray? I study a rose, I count the stars, I marvel at the beauty of creation and how perfectly ordered it is, at man, the most beautiful work of the Creator, his brain thirsting for knowledge, his heart for love, and his senses, all his senses alert or gratified.’ "<br /></i><br />Hasan-i-Sabah is the one who started the 'order of assassins' - the expert murders; from whose popularity the English words for assassination and assassin are driven; and which also seem to hold genes for suicidal terrorist bombers.<br /><blockquote>
  <i><br />How can precautions be taken against a man intent on dying? All protection is based upon dissuasion, and we know that important personages are surrounded by an imposing guard whose role is to make any potential attacker fear inevitable death. But what if the attacker is not afraid of dying, and has been convinced that martyrdom is a short-cut to paradise?<br /></i>
</blockquote><br />The book deals with the fate of a book having the same name as the tile of the book - Samarkand which is the only copy of the manuscript in which Omar wrote his poems (the ones that are popular now only survive in memories of people, who had seen this manuscript.<br /><br />Some Quotes<br /><br />A ruler when announcing the end of rule of ultra-religious 'Order of Assassins':<br /><blockquote>
  <i><br />"Since we are now in Paradise and in permanent contact with the Creator, we no have any need to address Him at fixed times; those who persist in making the five prayers show thereby how little they believe in the Resurrection. Prayer has become an act of unbelief.’ <br /></i>
</blockquote><br /><blockquote>
  <i><br />"Cancer, cancer, cancer,’ he repeated as if in warning. ‘In the past doctors attributed illnesses to the conjunctions of the stars, but only cancer has kept its astrological name, in all languages. The fear is still there.’"<br /></i>
</blockquote><br />An admirable example of political tolerance in Persia: <br /><blockquote>
  <i><br />"Taking refuge, or taking bast as the Persians say, means giving oneself over to a strictly passive resistance in the shelter of a sanctuary of which there were several in the area of Teheran: the mausoleum of Shah Adbul-Azim, the royal stables, and the smallest bast of all, the wheeled cannon in Topkhane Square – if a fugitive clung to it, the forces of order no longer had any right to lay hands on him."<br /></i>
</blockquote><br />And finally, there is no other way to end a book on Omar except with one of his poems:<br /><blockquote>
  <i><br />"We are the pawns, and Heaven is the player; <br />This is plain truth, and not a mode of speech. <br />We move about the chessboard of the world. <br />Then drop into the casket of the void. "<br /></i>
</blockquote></span>
  <a data-text-id="1756318811747443799" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_2305410013" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2305410013" />
    <a title="Bob Newman" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/72341080-bob-newman"><img alt="Bob Newman" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2305410013?book_show_action=true">Feb 22, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bob Newman" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bob Newman" href="/user/show/72341080-bob-newman">Bob Newman</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/72341080-bob-newman?shelf=arab-world">arab-world</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/72341080-bob-newman?shelf=european-literature">european-literature</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2305410013" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2797423173017818403">"The Red Violin" meets Omar Khayyam on board the Titanic !<br/><br/>Well, OK, maybe my title is stretching things a little, but not by much. This is a very original novel which risked getting out of control, but did not. Only a very skilled writer could pull things together the way Amin Maalouf has done in SAMARKAND. The title refers to the ancient Central Asian city, now in Uzbekistan, where the novel opens with Omar Khayyam, the renowned poet (also mathematician and astrologer), receiving a large blank </span>
  <span id="freeText2797423173017818403" style="display:none">"The Red Violin" meets Omar Khayyam on board the Titanic !<br /><br />Well, OK, maybe my title is stretching things a little, but not by much. This is a very original novel which risked getting out of control, but did not. Only a very skilled writer could pull things together the way Amin Maalouf has done in SAMARKAND. The title refers to the ancient Central Asian city, now in Uzbekistan, where the novel opens with Omar Khayyam, the renowned poet (also mathematician and astrologer), receiving a large blank volume from a high city official. Omar spends his life filling it with verses and with his accumulated wisdom. Others write his biography in the margins. Like the violin in the movie "The Red Violin", the book of Khayyam's work ties together an unlikely collection of characters to be found in Central Asia and Iran during the 11th and 12th centuries, as well as in late 19th and early 20th century Iran and Istanbul. Americans play a prominent role. There is romance, there is suspense and the Titanic is really in there ! I was surprised by the strong feeling of suspense--my desire to know what happened--because the main characters are not that well-developed in a conventional literary sense, yet, as with the protagonists of legends and tales, we identify strongly with them. The details of history of the earlier period are little known, but Maalouf takes the basic facts and turns them into a novel that recalls Persian miniatures. Princes, ladies, assassins, and ministers prance across the pages, colorful, larger than life, always doomed. Love, success, power, faith, and intrigue--all destined to become dust in this magic shadow box of the World.<br />I can't say that Maalouf's writing reminds me of anyone in particular. This book is one of a kind. It's an enjoyable, readable novel that I recommend to anyone who doesn't mind absorbing a lot of foreign names and new cultural concepts.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2797423173017818403" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/29160274-czarny-pies?shelf=french-lit">french-lit</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1756783143" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15055926927575720821">Samarkand is a reflection on the role of Islam in the modern world written by a member of the Académie française which is one of the great bastions of world civilization. The author who believes that sex and literature are the only true good things in life believes that Islam is a positive force only on the rare occasions when it encourages both. <br/>As he was writing, Maalouf was likely thinking of the civil war that was ongoing in his native Lebanon. However, he chose to set his novel in Uzbekstan</span>
  <span id="freeText15055926927575720821" style="display:none">Samarkand is a reflection on the role of Islam in the modern world written by a member of the Académie française which is one of the great bastions of world civilization. The author who believes that sex and literature are the only true good things in life believes that Islam is a positive force only on the rare occasions when it encourages both. <br />As he was writing, Maalouf was likely thinking of the civil war that was ongoing in his native Lebanon. However, he chose to set his novel in Uzbekstan roughly 3500 kilometres from Lebanon. <br /> Maalouf begins with a biography of the 11th century Persian poet Omar Khayyam. Omar Khayyam was a scientist and a teacher with two close friends, Nizam al-Mulk a vizier of the Seljuq Empire and Hassan ibn al-Sabbah, the founder of the Ismaeli sect of the assassins. Omar is not attracted either to riches like Nizam al-Mulk or to ascetic virtue like Hassan ibn al-Sabbah. Omar’s only interests are women, alcohol and knowledge. Unfortunately the activities of his two friends create considerable obstacles for Omar in his pursuit of happiness. The modern reader will unquestionably feel great sympathy for Omar.<br />After having spent the first half of the book examining political and cultural conflicts within the Muslim world, Maalouf turns his attention to the phenomenon of Western Imperialism in Islamic countries in the second half of the novel. The case study he uses is the Iranian Constitutional Revolution of 1906 in which progressive liberals succeed in obtaining a constitutional monarchy. In this section of this book, Maalouf shows the western powers supporting a corrupt regime to thwart the aims of the progressive liberals attmepting to modernize the country. <br />Between the first and second halves of the book, Maalouf manages to address all the issues currently raging on the topic of Islam and the West. The problem is how to link the two halves in a coherent novel. Maalouf’s solution is to view the 1906 Constitutional Revolution through the eyes of an American visiting Iran in search of a Manuscript of the Quatrain transcribed by none other than Omar Khayyam himself. Strangely enough this artificial device actually works.<br />Maalouf is currently on the sidelines observing the civil war in Syria between ISIS and the Ba’ath party screaming a “Curse on both your houses.” If you read his novel, you will understand why he feels the way he does. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="15055926927575720821" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_289239325" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/289239325" />
    <a title="Krista the Krazy Kataloguer" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/316102-krista-the-krazy-kataloguer"><img alt="Krista the Krazy Kataloguer" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1191519600p2/316102.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/289239325?book_show_action=true">Jul 10, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Krista the Krazy Kataloguer" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Krista the Krazy Kataloguer" href="/user/show/316102-krista-the-krazy-kataloguer">Krista the Krazy Kataloguer</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/316102-krista-the-krazy-kataloguer?shelf=read-historical-fiction">read-historical-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/316102-krista-the-krazy-kataloguer?shelf=read-adult-fiction">read-adult-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer289239325" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4150718728151540178">I actually want to give this 3 1/2 stars--4 stars for the first half of the book, 3 stars for the second half.<br/><br/>This my second novel by Maalouf, and I have to say that I liked <i>The Rock of Tanios</i> better. With <i>Samarkand</i> I felt like I was reading two different novels. It was divided into four books, the first two of which were a fictional life of the 11th-century Persian poet Omar Khayyam, narrated in the third person. It was fascinating, as I had no idea he was an astrologer/astronomer, philosopher,</span>
  <span id="freeText4150718728151540178" style="display:none">I actually want to give this 3 1/2 stars--4 stars for the first half of the book, 3 stars for the second half.<br /><br />This my second novel by Maalouf, and I have to say that I liked <i>The Rock of Tanios</i> better. With <i>Samarkand</i> I felt like I was reading two different novels. It was divided into four books, the first two of which were a fictional life of the 11th-century Persian poet Omar Khayyam, narrated in the third person. It was fascinating, as I had no idea he was an astrologer/astronomer, philosopher, and scientist as well as a poet. I loved the description of places, foods, customs, clothing, and politics. <br /><br />The last two books were a first person narrative, told from the point of view of Benjamin Lesage, a late 19th-century Omar Khayyam enthusiast who makes several trips to Persia in search of the original manuscript of Khayyam's <i>The Rubaiyat</i>, which also contains in the margins a life of Khayyam and a description of the political situation during his time. Apparently, verses were written after his death and wrongly attributed to him. In the course of searching for this book to determine which were the verses truly written by Khayyam, Lesage becomes involved in local politics and Persia's fight for a democratic government and a break from British and Russian domination. Maalouf certainly does his research, as I learned more than I wanted to know about how Persia/Iran came to be the way it is today. <br /><br />An introduction of sorts provides the reason for the two separate narratives. It is revealed that the only original manuscript of Omar Khayyam's <i>The Rubaiyat</i> went down with the Titanic. The first narrative of Khayyam's life explains how <i>The Rubaiyat</i> came to be written. The second narrative set in the 19th century explains what happened to the manuscript after Khayyam died and how it ended up on the Titanic. I think the second narrative is written in the first person in order to draw the reader in to a story that might otherwise be boring, with its emphasis on politics. <br /><br />The novel certainly had an interesting structure, but it made story seem a bit disjointed. The switch from the 11th-12th centuries to the late 19th was too abrupt. While I zipped through the life of Khayyam, I labored through the second half. Nevertheless, I do have to recommend the book, especially if you're interested in Khayyam or the history of Persia/Iran. Now I'll have to pick up my copy of <i>The Rubaiyat</i> again.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4150718728151540178" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2065655516" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2065655516" />
    <a title="Inderjit Sanghera" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/900800-inderjit-sanghera"><img alt="Inderjit Sanghera" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1564826235p2/900800.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2065655516?book_show_action=true">Aug 20, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Inderjit Sanghera" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Inderjit Sanghera" href="/user/show/900800-inderjit-sanghera">Inderjit Sanghera</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2065655516" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4176700106284543587">‘Samarkand’ tells the story of Omar Khayyam, Persian polymath and his namesake from 900 hundred years later, an American scholar whose life becomes entangled in incipient Iranian independence movement. The novels represents the higher echelon of historical fiction; informative and at times captivating and suffused with (at times a somewhat sententious) discourse on religious tolerance and dogmatism. As a work of art, however, it’s characters are at times too embedded in the tropes and clichés wh</span>
  <span id="freeText4176700106284543587" style="display:none">‘Samarkand’ tells the story of Omar Khayyam, Persian polymath and his namesake from 900 hundred years later, an American scholar whose life becomes entangled in incipient Iranian independence movement. The novels represents the higher echelon of historical fiction; informative and at times captivating and suffused with (at times a somewhat sententious) discourse on religious tolerance and dogmatism. As a work of art, however, it’s characters are at times too embedded in the tropes and clichés which are a necessary part of historical fiction. </span>
  <a data-text-id="4176700106284543587" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

I would suggest drinking this book as a shot, chased by My Name Is Red by Pamuk.<br/><br/>But seriously, students of Islamic history, especially those modern-day Hashishin, will want to read this book, both on its own merits, as well as a decompression from all of those heady, repetitive history books. Take a break, let your mind wander around in this place for a while...</span>

<div id="review_723957141" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/723957141" />
    <a title="Farhan Khalid" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2881911-farhan-khalid"><img alt="Farhan Khalid" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1350661583p2/2881911.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/723957141?book_show_action=true">Sep 20, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Farhan Khalid" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Farhan Khalid" href="/user/show/2881911-farhan-khalid">Farhan Khalid</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2881911-farhan-khalid?shelf=middle-east">middle-east</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2881911-farhan-khalid?shelf=history">history</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2881911-farhan-khalid?shelf=novel">novel</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2881911-farhan-khalid?shelf=iran">iran</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2881911-farhan-khalid?shelf=political">political</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2881911-farhan-khalid?shelf=poetry">poetry</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer723957141" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13702616756585602545"><b>Samarkand</b>, the most beautiful face the Earth has ever turned towards the sun<br/><br/>I have had to distance myself from people in order to hear the voice of my memory, to nurture a naive hope and insistent vision<br/><br/>I am not one of those for whom faith is simply fear of judgement<br/><br/>How do I pray? <br/><br/>I study a rose, I count the stars, I marvel at the beauty of creation and how perfectly ordered it is<br/><br/>We live in the age of the secret and of fear<br/><br/>You must have two faces<br/><br/>Show one to the crowd, and keep the other for your</span>
  <span id="freeText13702616756585602545" style="display:none"><b>Samarkand</b>, the most beautiful face the Earth has ever turned towards the sun<br /><br />I have had to distance myself from people in order to hear the voice of my memory, to nurture a naive hope and insistent vision<br /><br />I am not one of those for whom faith is simply fear of judgement<br /><br />How do I pray? <br /><br />I study a rose, I count the stars, I marvel at the beauty of creation and how perfectly ordered it is<br /><br />We live in the age of the secret and of fear<br /><br />You must have two faces<br /><br />Show one to the crowd, and keep the other for yourself and your Creator<br /><br />She lifted it a little more and cast a glance which Omar noticed<br /><br />It was a moment too fleet to be detected by the crowd but an eternity for the lover<br /><br />Time has two faces, Khayyam said to himself<br /><br />It has two dimensions, its length is measured by the rhythm of the sun but its depth by the rhythm of passion<br /><br />Poetry lives in complete calm through the centuries<br /><br />The only veil I am wearing is the night<br /><br />How many nights had fate granted them?<br /><br />Their every kiss carried with it a taste of farewell, their every embrace a breathless flight<br /><br />God has made us fall in love just as he has made certain flowers poisonous<br /><br />I knew that I was going to cry this evening, but I did not know I would cry such bitter tears<br /><br />Look at me, Omar<br /><br />Look at me for the last time! <br /><br />Remember, I am your lover<br /><br />You loved me and I loved you<br /><br />Can you still recognize me?<br /><br />You can hide your writings, but not your tears<br /><br />I will not change my route because of my name, and I will not change my name because of my route<br /><br />The idea had been waiting for him there<br /><br />Both Paradise and Hell are in you<br /><br />Together, and different<br /><br />For Omar, life was different<br /><br />It was the pleasure of science and the science of pleasure<br /><br />Life is like a fire<br /><br />Flames which the passer-by forgets. Ashes which the wind scatters<br /><br />Do not look back, do not say goodbye, do not even collect your belongings<br /><br />You will compose your poems and I shall observe my stars<br /><br />The world will cease to exist for us<br /><br />The wind from <b>Isfahan</b> carried a sharp perfume of apricot. But how lifeless were the streets!<br /><br />There was no music, not a sound, no secrets<br /><br />-<br /><br />It could just be a simple coincidence that we were reading the same book<br /><br />Omar appeared to us instantly like fate’s password<br /><br />If sweetness had a colour, it would be hers<br /><br />God, she was beautiful<br /><br />My first image of the Orient – a woman such as only the desert poet knew how to praise: <br /><br />Her face was the sun, they would have said<br /><br />Her hair the protecting shadow, her eyes fountains of cool water, her body the most slender of palm-trees and her smile a mirage<br /><br />In the cities of the Orient, one always looks for the colours of the present and the shades of the past<br /><br /><b>Teheran</b> had too short a history!<br /><br />We debated and constructed a thousand gloomy futures<br /><br />The future was no more than a forbidden dream<br /><br />Poems which dealt with a wisdom which was so accurate or a beauty so timeless<br /><br />There were words of heartache and consolation, the touching monologue of a defeated and dignified poet<br /><br />But there were also words of joy and sublime<br /><br />She looked deep into my eyes, as if she were reading an open book<br /><br />She had understood everything and sighed<br /><br />Once freed, <b>Samarkand</b> will be able to tell us its history<br /><br />Everything from that time has been destroyed – as if by a curse<br /><br />Walls, palaces, orchards, gardens and books<br /><br />There is the city which is buried under the ground<br /><br /><b>Samarkand</b> thus became the symbol of the inescapable meeting between man and his destiny<br /><br />The Orient, the place of all wonders and unfathomable nostalgia<br /><br />Shireen!<br /><br />Was it the way I uttered her name?<br /><br />The Titanic was now in a vertical position and its lights had dimmed<br /><br />The manuscript was our reason for being together<br /><br />Today I wonder: Did she exist? <br /><br />Was she anything other than the fruit of my oriental obsessions?<br /><br />The Rubaiyaat on the Titanic! <br /><br />Khayyam, if you could only see what a beautiful moment has been granted to us!</span>
  <a data-text-id="13702616756585602545" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_2883718281" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2883718281" />
    <a title="Z" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/97868261-z"><img alt="Z" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1562328165p2/97868261.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2883718281?book_show_action=true">Jul 05, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Z" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Z" href="/user/show/97868261-z">Z</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback " href="/book/show/311690.Samarcande">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/97868261-z?shelf=lebanon">lebanon</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/97868261-z?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2883718281" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16270280021502893774">If you have never read Amin Maalouf before, then I recommend starting with "Samarkand". It contains real historical events, real characters and rich of details, and reading it is a real pleasure.<br/><p> <b> The novel is divided into two parts: </b> <br/>  1- The story happened in the 11th century and which tells the era of Nizam Al-Mulk and the everyday life of Omar Al-Khayam(the poet).<br/> 2- The second is in 19 century where Benjamin O. Lesage (a passionate about quatrains of Omar AL Khayam) who tells us his quest f</p></span>
  <span id="freeText16270280021502893774" style="display:none">If you have never read Amin Maalouf before, then I recommend starting with "Samarkand". It contains real historical events, real characters and rich of details, and reading it is a real pleasure.<br /><p> <b> The novel is divided into two parts: </b> <br />  1- The story happened in the 11th century and which tells the era of Nizam Al-Mulk and the everyday life of Omar Al-Khayam(the poet).<br /> 2- The second is in 19 century where Benjamin O. Lesage (a passionate about quatrains of Omar AL Khayam) who tells us his quest for the lost manuscript of the poet. <br /><br />**********************************<br /> Like a pleasant voyage, "Samarkand" is a captivating novel that I had read with constant passion and curiosity. <br />I don't remember feeling bored during my reading, I was completely soaked in the Persian ambience and was dreaming of lying under an orange tree in one of the Persian gardens; thanks to Amin Maalouf's mastery of the description. </p><br />Throughout the chapters, you will find some verses of the quatrains of Omar AL-khayam (Ar-Rubaä'iyat - الرباعيات). <br />Although, I'm not sure these quatrains are the authentic ones. I have read that several poets have attributed their poems to Al-Khayyam for fear of being convicted because of their blasphemous content. <br />In short, everything that is published today in the name of Omar Alkhayam is not necessarily his.<br /><br /> <i> <b> PS : (without doubting the research and credibility of the author). </b> </i></span>
  <a data-text-id="16270280021502893774" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/71088750-nouha-abbas-chohra?shelf=historical-fiction">historical-fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2832919165" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8392990776766710948">This is my first Amine Maalouf book and it is already at the top of " my favourite books of all times" list for I was deeply absorbed in the story .<br/>It follows the story of an 11th century Persian poet and philosopher Omar Khayyam(whom poetry is only a tiny part of his life) with two close friends, Nizam al-Mulk a vizier of the Seljuq Empire and Hassan ibn al-Sabbah, the founder of the Ismaeeli sect of the assassins. Omar is not attracted either to wealth like Nizam al-Mulk or to ascetic virtue l</span>
  <span id="freeText8392990776766710948" style="display:none">This is my first Amine Maalouf book and it is already at the top of " my favourite books of all times" list for I was deeply absorbed in the story .<br />It follows the story of an 11th century Persian poet and philosopher Omar Khayyam(whom poetry is only a tiny part of his life) with two close friends, Nizam al-Mulk a vizier of the Seljuq Empire and Hassan ibn al-Sabbah, the founder of the Ismaeeli sect of the assassins. Omar is not attracted either to wealth like Nizam al-Mulk or to ascetic virtue like Hassan ibn al-Sabbah. Omar’s only interests are women, alcohol and knowledge. Unfortunately the activities of his two friends create considerable obstacles for Omar in his pursuit of happiness. <br />In the second half of the book , Maalouf tries to address all the issues currently raging on the topic of Islam and the West. The problem is how to link the two halves in a coherent novel. Maalouf’s solution is to view the 1906 Constitutional Revolution through the eyes of an American named Benjamin O. Lesage visiting Iran in search of the (fictional ) original Manuscript of the Rubaiates transcribed by none other than Omar Khayyam himself. <br />I adored it ! This work is worth your time ! I would highly recommend it for everyone <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="8392990776766710948" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2262501215" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7178411704160044197">This book started off great for me. While I generally don't read poetry, I really appreciate Omar Khayyam's work - it's pure, powerful philosophy. I still can remember some elements, even if I've read them in high school last time. <br/>Thus, I was more than interested in a book about Khayyam, one of the luminaries of his generation. And that part was great - the three main characters, the history, the politics, the vivid descriptions of life and belief. I couldn't ask more from a book about this per</span>
  <span id="freeText7178411704160044197" style="display:none">This book started off great for me. While I generally don't read poetry, I really appreciate Omar Khayyam's work - it's pure, powerful philosophy. I still can remember some elements, even if I've read them in high school last time. <br />Thus, I was more than interested in a book about Khayyam, one of the luminaries of his generation. And that part was great - the three main characters, the history, the politics, the vivid descriptions of life and belief. I couldn't ask more from a book about this period. I will definitely look into all of the people described in this part of the book. <br />In a way, I wish I would have stopped there. The second part falls quite flat (for me). The main character is rather bland and uninterested. He tells us he is interested in things, or else we would have no idea. The love story is cliched. The love interest is just as bland. In the first part, Djahane has flaws and personality and gets what she wants. Shireen however is undeveloped. I like to read about the political turmoil at the time, there are some interesting characters, but I am forced to spend too much time with these two. And it's not connected to the fact that he is American, all the other Americans are quite interesting. <br />Overall, considering the real book was lost in the Titanic disaster, it is a big temptation to write a book about it. However, in this case, I think a book about the lives and times of Kayyam would have been enough.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer8229374366477435348">*Score: 8.5/10*<br/><br/>Synopsis: The setting is Persia across 10th-11th century and then late 19th century. The novel aims to highlight key events in Persian history, while also putting focus on the character Omar Khayyam and his influence on science and astronomy as well as his Rubaiyat poetry collection.<br/><br/>Pros:<br/><br/>- The first half, focusing on Omar Khayyam era and the Assassins is absolutely brilliant, with a really vivid historical setting and atmosphere<br/>- Good mix of storytelling and historical knowledge<br/>- </span>
  <span id="freeText8229374366477435348" style="display:none">*Score: 8.5/10*<br /><br />Synopsis: The setting is Persia across 10th-11th century and then late 19th century. The novel aims to highlight key events in Persian history, while also putting focus on the character Omar Khayyam and his influence on science and astronomy as well as his Rubaiyat poetry collection.<br /><br />Pros:<br /><br />- The first half, focusing on Omar Khayyam era and the Assassins is absolutely brilliant, with a really vivid historical setting and atmosphere<br />- Good mix of storytelling and historical knowledge<br />- Makes you interested in many historical figures and in Persian history<br />- Entertaining without sacrificing depth<br /><br />Cons:<br /><br />- The second half, told from prespective of an American French who is obsessed with Persian history, is less interesting, and loses focus on the essance<br />- Second half has too much Politics, which was not very interesting for me personally<br />- The style changes sometimes to be too much like academic history instead of storytelling within a historical setting.<br /><br />Overall: Even though I am not a big fan of the second half, overall this is still a highly readable, entertaining, and informative read. Omar Khayyam and Hasan Sabbah are fascinating characters, and the section on Alamut made me want to read much more about it. I would have scored this 8.5 easily if it maintained quality and focus of first half, but regardless this is still a 4 star read and recommended for those interested in the subject matter</span>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">everyone who like novels  based on historical fact.</span></div>

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/7408130-sylvia?shelf=challenge-2012">challenge-2012</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer319693120" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11887686158393461198">A great story! I learned about the Rubaiyat, because I'm also interested in books about the Titanic disaster. More than once I read that the Rubaiyat had been on board. If it's true; I haven't figured that out yet. Some say yes it was, other say no it's hoax, the manuscript has vanished centuries ago. <br/><br/>And now this great story. The first two part about the creation of the book and it's incredible travels are wonderful. Not only to know what happened to the book, but also to learn about Persia dur</span>
  <span id="freeText11887686158393461198" style="display:none">A great story! I learned about the Rubaiyat, because I'm also interested in books about the Titanic disaster. More than once I read that the Rubaiyat had been on board. If it's true; I haven't figured that out yet. Some say yes it was, other say no it's hoax, the manuscript has vanished centuries ago. <br /><br />And now this great story. The first two part about the creation of the book and it's incredible travels are wonderful. Not only to know what happened to the book, but also to learn about Persia during time now long forgotten. <br />And then the last two parts. More contemporary, but as interesting and even more a page turner, because the historical events are spreading their long shadows up till today I think. I wonder, if the domination of Western countries in the Iranian/Persian affairs are still of influence in nowadays politics. I might be wrong, but I know from my history study, that after the decline of the Ottoman Empire the dominating Western countries divided the Middle-East in sphere of influence, mainly to protect their own economics and colonial imperialism. <br /><br />This book is historical fiction and the antagonists are no real people, but the book was composed so well, that I thought I was reading a novel, that happened the way Amin Maalouf has written it. <br /><br />I'm going to find a translation of the Rubaiyat and see if those quatrains will inspire me. <br /><br /><br /></span>

    <a title="Ty" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/59818-ty"><img alt="Ty" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1527614750p2/59818.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/926952212?book_show_action=true">May 03, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ty" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ty" href="/user/show/59818-ty">Ty</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780349106168" href="/book/show/979196.Samarkand">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer926952212" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15186995472422398574">The first half of this book takes place in the ninth century. It's about the famous Sufi poet Omar Khayyam, and two of his friends/enemies. I kept thinking it was going to have some important things to say about people who want political power vs people who want religious power vs people who just want to write poems and drink wine. It didn't really, but I still liked it. It made me want to go to Iran, especially <a href='http://www.google.com/images?q=esfahan' rel='nofollow noopener'>Esfahan</a>, and there were some lines I liked:<br/><p>
  <br/>
  <i>"I despise the zeal of the devout, </i></p></span>
  <span id="freeText15186995472422398574" style="display:none">The first half of this book takes place in the ninth century. It's about the famous Sufi poet Omar Khayyam, and two of his friends/enemies. I kept thinking it was going to have some important things to say about people who want political power vs people who want religious power vs people who just want to write poems and drink wine. It didn't really, but I still liked it. It made me want to go to Iran, especially <a href="http://www.google.com/images?q=esfahan" rel="nofollow noopener">Esfahan</a>, and there were some lines I liked:<br /><p>
  <br />
  <i>"I despise the zeal of the devout, but I have never said that the One was two."<br /><br />"What do you want me to say? These things are veiled, and you and I are on the same side of the veil. When it falls, we will no longer be here."<br /><br />"My God, you know that I have sought to perceive you as much as I could. Forgive me if my knowledge of you has been my only path towards you!"</i>
  <br />
</p><p><br /><br />The book isn't as Serious as those quotes might make it sound, and the second half is even less Serious. It's an adventure story about trying to recover a manuscript of Omar Khayyam's <i>Rubaiyaat</i> during Iran's Consitutional Revolution of 1905, and it ends, sort of annoyingly, with the sinking of the Titanic. The bookstore here has several of Maalouf's other books available in English, and I'll probably read them soon.</p></span>
  <a data-text-id="15186995472422398574" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer15518480718571449407">There are so many reasons why I loved this book: 1. It's very well written. 2. It's a great source of information about so many historical events that had gone through centuries. By reading this book I have learned some history from the turn of the second millennium about Omar El Khayyam, The Assassins and the Seljuk Empire.   <span id="freeText15518480718571449407" style="display:none">There are so many reasons why I loved this book: 1. It's very well written. 2. It's a great source of information about so many historical events that had gone through centuries. By reading this book I have learned some history from the turn of the second millennium about Omar El Khayyam, The Assassins and the Seljuk Empire. Then I have also learned about the Persian constitutional revolution at the turn of the last century in the second millennium. And last, I have also learned a little about Titanic and its unexpected fate. Through reading this book I have seen how history always repeats itself and we never learn. There is so much in common between our revolution here in Egypt and the constitutional revolution, one thing is how the military men and old regime used the mullahs (sheiks) to brainwash people and tell them that the constitution is against Islam. 3. It's a very very enjoyable book that even though it tells stories of very hard times was still peaceful and comfortable to read, especially with Khayyam's poetry popping up every now and then. I'd recommend it to anyone who would want to know about the history of the Assassins, and what they called back then "the dawn of the Orient" yes, something like the Arab spring.</span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3412731733" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12450265542677220077">I had to read this book because of Omar Khayyam. A skillfully told tale between two times. The first in the times of Omar Khayyam; the great Persian mathematician, astrologist and poet and the story of the birth of his Rubaiyat. The 11th century Persia, when the cities of Samarkand and Bukhara were the greatest in the world. It is in Samarkand that Khayyam starts to compose the quatrains whose collection constitutes the immortal Rubaiyat. And in it he wrote:<br/><br/> “Samarkand, the most beautiful face t</span>
  <span id="freeText12450265542677220077" style="display:none">I had to read this book because of Omar Khayyam. A skillfully told tale between two times. The first in the times of Omar Khayyam; the great Persian mathematician, astrologist and poet and the story of the birth of his Rubaiyat. The 11th century Persia, when the cities of Samarkand and Bukhara were the greatest in the world. It is in Samarkand that Khayyam starts to compose the quatrains whose collection constitutes the immortal Rubaiyat. And in it he wrote:<br /><br /> “Samarkand, the most beautiful face the Earth has ever turned towards the sun”.<br /><br />The author's descriptions of the courts, the Bazaars, the life of kings is woven into a mystic tale; paralleling the fable of Hassan Bin Sabah, and the origin of his Assassins. I totally got lost in this story, the imagination was strong, the characters powerful with a bit of romance and hurt. <br />The second part of the book showcases the love of an American for Omar Khayyam, driven by love and curiosity, and a desire to find the lost manuscript of Omar Khayyam, and for that, he travels to Persia for two times, but got immersed in the ongoing dilemma of parliament that was established for the first time in Iran. The second half didn't appeal much, it was burdened with details I didn't wanna read after just going through the wonderful life of Omar Khayyam. <br /><br /><i> “We are the pawns, and Heaven is the player;<br />This is plain truth, and not a mode of speech.<br />We move about the chessboard of the world.<br />Then drop into the casket of the void.<br />And<br />Arise, we have eternity for sleeping. </i></span>
  <a data-text-id="12450265542677220077" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Kevin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/13827890-kevin"><img alt="Kevin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1386143953p2/13827890.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/475164660?book_show_action=true">Dec 11, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kevin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kevin" href="/user/show/13827890-kevin">Kevin</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13827890-kevin?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer475164660" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7116669363737273772">Paperback: 312 pages<br/>Publisher: Interlink Publishing Group (September 1, 2003)<br/>Language: English<br/>ISBN-10: 1566562937<br/>ISBN-13: 978-1566562935<br/><br/>The story of Samarkand is very long, not by the count of pages, but by the extent of time in history that it takes us through. Obviously, as the name suggests, it's the story of 'the philosopher of the world' and a great poet from the East, Omar Khayyam. The book is written in four parts, as it takes us through many emotional moments in the saga of Persian histo</span>
  <span id="freeText7116669363737273772" style="display:none">Paperback: 312 pages<br />Publisher: Interlink Publishing Group (September 1, 2003)<br />Language: English<br />ISBN-10: 1566562937<br />ISBN-13: 978-1566562935<br /><br />The story of Samarkand is very long, not by the count of pages, but by the extent of time in history that it takes us through. Obviously, as the name suggests, it's the story of 'the philosopher of the world' and a great poet from the East, Omar Khayyam. The book is written in four parts, as it takes us through many emotional moments in the saga of Persian history, clash of cultures, war, triumph and defeat, and serene love. It starts with the poets' early days, his love affair, solitude and divine life. <br />'Samarkand' stories seem to end midway, when it suddenly takes us to the tales of triumphant exploration on the manuscript of 'Rubaiyat', Khayyam's master piece work to the world, which was 'hidden' for centuries as it had changed hands many a time. The manuscript remains intact, even after the invasion by the Mongol, Genghis Khan and subsequently setting ablaze Persia's best libraries. <br />The manuscript of Samarkand - "Rubaiyat" ends up in the hands of beautiful princess, Shireen, grand daughter of Shah of Persia, who later reveals it to her long time lover. They read together the quatrains of the text in their secret rendezvous, the sublime beauty of which could only be cherished with spirit and beauty at your side!<br /><br />"Some wine! Let it be as pink as your cheeks<br />And my regrets as light as your locks"<br /><br />Annals from the history of Orient, the scented petals of love as these, help cleanse our souls and intoxicate our spirits to last....for ever!<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="7116669363737273772" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('Dk9ielFW+Dy4Z8cMKS0wi6DdBSv46I8POJag4H/y5sPLd7lsdewtyk3SEsMLSewwb0v5A0zf30pqqGuN6ZhsjQ==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

<script>
//<![CDATA[
      var newTip = new Tip($('bookCover_171970'), "\n\n  <h2><a class=\"readable bookTitle\" href=\"https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/171970.Alamut?from_choice=false&amp;from_home_module=false\">Alamut<\/a><\/h2>\n\n      <div>\n        by <a class=\"authorName\" href=\"/author/show/100326.Vladimir_Bartol?a=9&amp;origin=related_works\">Vladimir Bartol<\/a>\n      <\/div>\n\n          <div class=\"smallText uitext darkGreyText\">\n            <span class=\"minirating\"><span class=\"stars staticStars notranslate\"><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p10\"><\/span><span size=\"12x12\" class=\"staticStar p3\"><\/span><\/span> 4.31 avg rating &mdash; 9,034 ratings<\/span>            &mdash; published 1938\n          <\/div>\n\n    <div class=\"addBookTipDescription\">\n      \n<span id=\"freeTextContainer16017892335743176060\">Alamut takes place in 11th Century Persia, in the fortress of Alamut, where self-proclaimed prophet Hasan ibn Sabbah is setting up his mad but brilliant plan to rule the region with a handful elite fighters who are to become his \"living daggers.\" By <\/span>\n  <span id=\"freeText16017892335743176060\" style=\"display:none\">Alamut takes place in 11th Century Persia, in the fortress of Alamut, where self-proclaimed prophet Hasan ibn Sabbah is setting up his mad but brilliant plan to rule the region with a handful elite fighters who are to become his &quot;living daggers.&quot; By creating a virtual paradise at Alamut, filled with beautiful women, lush gardens, wine and hashish, Sabbah is able to convince his young fighters that they can reach paradise if they follow his commands.\nWith parallels to Osama bin Laden, Alamut tells the story of how Sabbah was able to instill fear into the ruling class by creating a small army of devotees who were willing to kill, and be killed, in order to achieve paradise. Believing in the supreme Ismaili motto “Nothing is true, everything is permitted,” Sabbah wanted to “experiment” with how far he could manipulate religious devotion for his own political gain through appealing to what he called the stupidity and gullibility of people and their passion for pleasure and selfish desires. \nThe novel focuses on Sabbah as he unveils his plan to his inner circle, and on two of his young followers — the beautiful slave girl Halima, who has come to Alamut to join Sabbah\'s paradise on earth, and young ibn Tahir, Sabbah\'s most gifted fighter. As both Halima and ibn Tahir become disillusioned with Sabbah\'s vision, their lives take unexpected turns. \n\nAlamut was originally written in 1938 as an allegory to Mussolini\'s fascist state. In the 1960\'s it became a cult favorite throughout Tito\'s Yugoslavia, and in the 1990s, during the Balkan\'s War, it was read as an allegory of the region\'s strife and became a bestseller in Germany, France and Spain. Following the attacks of September 11, 2001, the book once again took on a new life, selling more than 20,000 copies in a new Slovenian edition, and being translated around the world in more than 19 languages. This edition, translated by Michael Biggins, in the first-ever English translation.  <\/span>\n  <a data-text-id=\"16017892335743176060\" href=\"#\" onclick=\"swapContent(\$(this));; return Maya Duran is a single mother struggling to balance a demanding job at Istanbul University with the challenges of raising a teenage son. Her worries increase when she is tasked with looking after the enigmatic Maximilian Wagner, an elderly German-born Harvard professor visiting the city at the university\'s invitation.       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/36378.Amin_Maalouf">About Amin Maalouf</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer7355195254376878426">Amin Maalouf (Arabic: <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/4456125' rel='nofollow noopener'>أمين معلوف</a>; alternate spelling <i>Amin Maluf</i>) is a Lebanese journalist and novelist. He writes and publishes primarily in French.<br/><br/>Most of Maalouf's books have a historical setting, and like Umberto Eco, Orhan Pamuk, and Arturo Pérez-Reverte, Maalouf mixes fascinating historical facts with fantasy and philosophical ideas. In an interview Maalouf has said that his role as a writer </span>
  <span id="freeText7355195254376878426" style="display:none">Amin Maalouf (Arabic: <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/4456125" rel="nofollow noopener">أمين معلوف</a>; alternate spelling <i>Amin Maluf</i>) is a Lebanese journalist and novelist. He writes and publishes primarily in French.<br /><br />Most of Maalouf's books have a historical setting, and like Umberto Eco, Orhan Pamuk, and Arturo Pérez-Reverte, Maalouf mixes fascinating historical facts with fantasy and philosophical ideas. In an interview Maalouf has said that his role as a writer is to create "positive myths". Maalouf's works, written with the skill of a master storyteller, offer a sensitive view of the values and attitudes of different cultures in the Middle East, Africa and Mediterranean world.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7355195254376878426" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
</div>
